That the tritium is chemically bonded rather than being merely adsorbed trace amounts of tritium oxide from an unknown source is indicated by the fact that water and alcohol rinsing of the specimens removes less than $1\%$ of the tritium.

Several questions remain as to whether this phenomenon of chemisorption, if real, can be used to effect a separation of tritium from a molten-salt reactor. Notable among these questions is the apparently sluggish kinetics of the reaction. There does not yet appear to be a fundamental bar to the development of a process based on the observed phenomena.

# 6. Fluoroborate Chemistry

# 6.1 SOLUBILITY OF $\mathbf{BF}_3$ IN MOLTEN LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$

S. Cantor

As stated previously<sup>1</sup> the purpose of this study is to determine changes in fluoride ion activities in melts composed of LiF, $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ , and $\mathrm{ThF}_4$ . The working hypothesis in this investigation is that available or "free" fluoride ions combine with dissolved $\mathrm{BF}_3$ to form tetrafluoroborate ions:

$\mathbf{F}^{-}$ (in the melt) $+\mathrm{BF}_3$ (dissolved)

$$
\rightarrow \mathrm {B F} _ {4} ^ {-} (\text {i n t h e m e l t}).
$$

The results thus far reported<sup>1,2</sup> suggest that the above equilibrium is shifted almost completely to the right. Further, in $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}$ melts, the free fluoride concentration can be identified with the thermodynamic activity of LiF.

Solubility measurements have now been carried out in ternary solvents. The four compositions studied and the $\mathrm{BF}_3$ solubilities expressed in terms of Henry's law are listed in Table 6.1; also tabulated are the results of measurements in a binary (LiF-BeF $_2$ ) solvent.

The results are qualitatively consistent with observations in the $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}$ system, namely, the greater the LiF concentration (and activity) the greater the Henry's law constant. To calculate activities of LiF in the ternary solvents containing 85, 80, and 76 mole $\%$ LiF, liquidus temperatures were determined by the conventional thermal-halt method. In these three solvents, the activities of LiF are approximately proportional to the values of $K_{\mathrm{H}}$ , Henry's law constant. It should be noted,

however, that a similar proportionality between the systems $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}$ and $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ThF_4}$ does not seem to be the case. For instance, the activity of LiF in 79.7-20.3 mole $\%$ $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}$ is approximately 1.2 times that in 80-10-10 mole $\%$ $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ThF_4}$ ; the ratio of $K_{\mathrm{H}}$ for these two melts, which would be expected to be 1.2, is 2.3. The significance of this discrepancy is presently being explored.

# 6.2 SOLUBILITY OF $\mathbf{BF}_3$ IN MSBR PRIMARY SALT CONTAINING 8 MOLE \% NaF: SOME SAFETY CONSIDERATIONS

S. Cantor

The object of these measurements is to aid in assessing the consequences of a large inleakage of fluoroborate coolant into the primary system of an MSBR. In the preceding semiannual report, equations were presented with which to calculate $\mathrm{BF}_3$ partial pressures following a leak of coolant into the fuel salt. For a "small" leak, in which $1\mathrm{ft}^3$ of coolant mixes with $1720\mathrm{ft}^3$ [primary salt volume in a 1000-MW(e) MSBR] of fuel salt at $1250^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ , assuming only a $1\%$ void volume, we obtain an estimated $\mathrm{BF}_3$ pressure of 0.17 atm. The $\mathrm{BF}_3$ pressure would actually be less than this because of the large expansion volume of the off-gas system associated with the primary salt system.

Table 6.1. Solubility of ${\mathrm{{BF}}}^{3}$ in molten fluoride solvents   

<table><tr><td rowspan="2">Solvent (mole % LiF-BeF2-ThF4)</td><td rowspan="2">Temperature range measured (°C)</td><td rowspan="2">Liquidus temperature (°C)</td><td rowspan="2">Henry&#x27;s law constant vs temperature equationaKH in mole fraction BF3/atm; temperature in °K</td><td colspan="2">103KH</td></tr><tr><td>700°C</td><td>800°C</td></tr><tr><td>85-7.5-7.5</td><td>744–990</td><td>740</td><td>KH = exp (−13.297) exp (7065/T)</td><td>2.39b</td><td>1.21</td></tr><tr><td>80-10-10</td><td>700–967</td><td>660</td><td>= exp (−14.457) exp (7470/T)</td><td>1.13</td><td>0.555</td></tr><tr><td>79.7-20.3 (LiF-BeF2)</td><td>718–957</td><td>700c</td><td>= exp (−13.937) exp (7800/T)</td><td>2.68</td><td>1.27</td></tr><tr><td>76-12-12</td><td>647–907</td><td>601</td><td>= exp (−15.083) exp (7870/T)</td><td>0.915</td><td>0.431</td></tr><tr><td>72-16-12</td><td>530–795</td><td></td><td>= exp (−14.950) exp (7667/T)</td><td>0.849</td><td>0.407</td></tr></table>

$a$ Least-squares fit of the data.   
$b_{\mathrm{Extrapolated}}$   
$^{c}$ K. A. Romberger, J. Braunstein, and R. E. Thoma, J. Phys. Chem. 76, 1154 (1972).

Provided that the leak is not truly massive, when coolant leaks into MSBR fuel salt the $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ fraction of the coolant decomposes into NaF and $\mathrm{BF}_3$ . This NaF, along with the 8 mole $\%$ NaF of the coolant, will dissolve in the fuel salt, while the $\mathrm{BF}_3$ will be distributed between the salt phase and the available vapor space. Thus, by measuring the $\mathrm{BF}_3$ pressure in equilibrium with MSBR fuel salt containing NaF, one can estimate the partial pressure of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ that would be obtained following accidental mixing of coolant and fuel salts. In a melt consisting of 8 mole $\%$ NaF and 92 mole $\%$ fuel solvent (72-16-12 mole $\%$ LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$ ), solubilities were measured between 615 and $755^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ by the volumetric method previously described;² $\mathrm{BF}_3$ saturating pressures did not exceed 2 atm and Henry's law was obeyed. The data are represented by the equation

$$
K _ {\mathrm {H}} = \exp (- 1 5. 5 6 7 3) \exp \frac {9 2 0 3}{T \left(^ {\circ} \mathrm {K}\right)}, \tag {1}
$$

where $K_{\mathbf{H}}$ is the Henry's law constant in the units of mole fraction $\mathrm{BF}_3$ dissolved per atmosphere of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ pressure. At $1250^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ ( $677^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ) the solubility of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ in 8 mole % NaF-92 mole % fuel solvent is three times that in the fuel solvent alone.

The concentration of NaF in these measurements is equivalent to mixing $416\mathrm{ft}^3$ of fluoroborate coolant with the $1720\mathrm{ft}^3$ of fuel salt. The likelihood of such massive mixing of coolant and fuel must be considered extremely remote. There are now design provisions for draining the shell of a primary heat exchanger, so that even in the event of a double-ended tubing rupture, only a small fraction of the contents of a shell (whose volume is about $500\mathrm{ft}^3$ ) need leak into the primary salt circuit.

Nonetheless, an exploratory experiment has been performed by Richardson and Shaffer in which $\mathbf{BF}_3$ pressures were measured over a mixture of fuel salt and fluoroborate in the approximate volume ratio of 3:1. Their experiment was roughly equivalent to mixing 1.2 times the volume of coolant in the shell of one heat exchanger with the entire salt volume in the primary

system of a 1000-MW(e) MSBR. Assuming that the Henry's law constant for the melt (containing about 10 mole $\%$ NaF) in the Richardson-Shaffer experiment is $25\%$ greater than that given in Eq. (1) and applying this to the appropriate equations, we calculate a $\mathrm{BF}_3$ pressure at $647^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ of 201 psia; at this temperature, Richardson observed that the pressure was no less than 148.5 psia. Considering the approximations and assumptions (one being that Henry's law holds at a pressure of about 13 atm and with a $\mathrm{BF}_3$ concentration of about 6 mole $\%$ ), agreement between calculated and observed pressures is reasonably good.

# 6.3 CORROSION OF HASTELLOY N BY FLUOROBORATE MELTS

C.E.Bamberger C.F.Baes,Jr.

Previous measurements of the free energies of formation of $\mathrm{NaNiF_3}$ and $\mathrm{NaFeF_3^6}$ had allowed us to estimate the effect of redox potential on the oxidation of Ni and Fe in Hastelloy N by fluoroborate melts. Those estimates were based on measurements of the equilibrium

$$
\mathrm {N a M F} _ {3} (\mathrm {c}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {N a F} (\mathrm {d}) + \mathrm {M F} _ {2} (\mathrm {c}), K _ {1} = a _ {\mathrm {N a F}}, \tag {1}
$$

where $\mathbf{M}$ is either $\mathrm{Ni(II)}$ or $\mathrm{Fe(II)}$ , in molten fluoroborate. The activities of NaF were estimated by measuring the $\mathrm{BF}_3$ pressures generated by the equilibrium

$$
\mathrm {N a B F} _ {4} (\mathrm {d}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {N a F} (\mathrm {d}) + \mathrm {B F} _ {3} (\mathrm {g}), \tag {2}
$$

$$
K _ {2} = P _ {\mathrm {B F} 3} \frac {a _ {\mathrm {N a F}}}{a _ {\mathrm {N a B F} 4}},
$$

which has been measured by Cantor:

$$
\log K _ {2} = [ 5. 7 7 2 - 6. 5 1 3 (1 0 ^ {3} / T) ] \pm 0. 0 4.
$$

After termination of the reported experiments, 2 to 5 mm Hg of a mixture of mainly $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$ and $\mathrm{SiF}_{4}$ remained at room temperature, which raised questions regarding the purity of $\mathrm{BF}_{3}$ at the temperature range of the measurements (420-600°C). Consequently, measure

ments of equilibrium (1) were repeated using a modified system that permitted sampling of the gas phase at temperature for analyses by mass spectrometry. The concentration of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ was used to correct the measured pressures; only data where $\mathrm{BF}_3 \geqslant 92\%$ were used. When the $\mathrm{BF}_3$ concentration was below that limit, the system was purified by repeated evacuations at 25 to $200^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . The values of $P_{\mathrm{BF}_3}$ measured in millimeters of mercury in both equilibria ( $\mathrm{NiF}_2$ - $\mathrm{NaNiF}_3$ and $\mathrm{FeF}_2$ - $\mathrm{NaFeF}_3$ ) were corrected to millimeters of mercury at $0^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ and standard acceleration of gravity, and were fitted simultaneously by least squares to an expression

$$
P _ {\mathrm {B F} 3} = K _ {2} \left(\frac {1}{\mathrm {K} _ {1}} - \frac {1}{\gamma_ {\mathrm {N a F}}}\right) \tag {3}
$$

by assuming that $\log K_{1}$ varies linearly with temperature, that is, $\log K_{1} = a + b(10^{3} / T)$ , and that the entropies of formation of $\mathrm{NaNiF_3}$ and $\mathrm{NaFeF_3}$ [reaction (1)] are the same. Again, assuming that $\mathrm{NaF - NaBF_4}$ mixtures have an ideal entropy of mixing, from Cantor's results<sup>10</sup> we estimate

$$
\log \gamma_ {\mathrm {N a F}} = \left(\frac {1 1 3 . 9}{T}\right) \pm 0. 0 4.
$$

The results obtained are

$$
\log K _ {1} (\mathrm {N i}) = [ 0. 3 1 - 1. 9 1 (1 0 ^ {3} / T) ] \pm 0. 0 4, \tag {4a}
$$

$$
\log K _ {1} (\mathrm {F e}) = [ 0. 3 1 - 1. 6 8 (1 0 ^ {3} / T) ] \pm 0. 0 4 \tag {4b}
$$

and

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta G [ \text {r e a c t i o n} (1), \mathrm {N i} ] \\ = [ 8. 7 2 - 1. 4 2 (T / 1 0 ^ {3}) ] \pm 0. 2, \quad (5 a) \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta G [ \text {r e a c t i o n (1) , F e} ] \\ = [ 7. 6 8 - 1. 4 2 (T / 1 0 ^ {3}) ] \pm 0. 2. \quad (5 b) \\ \end{array}
$$

The agreement between measured and least-squares calculated values of $P_{\mathbf{BF}_3}$ is shown in Fig. 6.1. By

![](images/a6293cae5705739ecce9a583b549065b854456bde84ce79e066889442b9c0c22.jpg)  
Fig. 6.1. $\mathbf{BF}_3$ pressure generated by NaF-NaBF4 melts saturated with $\mathrm{NiF}_2\mathrm{-NaNiF}_3$ and $\mathrm{FeF_2}$ -NaFeF3. The lines were calculated by a least-squares fit of the data according to Eq. (3).

combining (5a) and (5b) with free energies of formation of $\mathrm{NiF}_2(\mathbf{c})$ and $\mathrm{FeF}_2(\mathbf{c})$ , respectively, known from Blood's measurements<sup>1</sup> and with $\Delta G^f$ of liquid $\mathrm{NaF}$ ,<sup>1,2</sup> we obtain

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta G ^ {f} \left[ \mathrm {N a N i F} _ {3} (\mathrm {c}) \right] \\ = \left[ - 2 9 5. 4 4 + 5 8. 4 9 (T / 1 0 ^ {3}) \right] \pm 1. 4, \quad (6 a) \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta G ^ {f} \left[ \mathrm {N a F e F} _ {3} (\mathrm {c}) \right] \\ = \left[ - 3 0 6. 6 9 + 5 3. 8 2 \left(T / 1 0 ^ {3}\right) \right] \pm 1. 4. \quad (6 b) \\ \end{array}
$$

Combining (4a) and (4b), respectively, with the values of equilibrium constants for the reduction of $\mathrm{NiF}_2(\mathbf{c})$ and $\mathrm{FeF}_2(\mathbf{c})$ by hydrogen, based on measurements by Blood, $^{11}$ we obtain the values of the equilibrium

![](images/8cf96fc2652bc3c9e7daf0370468db59f9f7a055b494dcbaa096215cfe138050.jpg)  
Fig. 6.2. Calculated HF particle pressures for the equilibrium (7) (in $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ -NaF eutectic).

constants of the reaction

$$
\begin{array}{l} 2 \mathrm {H F} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {M} (\text {H a s t e l l o y N}) \\ + \mathrm {N a F} (\mathrm {d}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {N a M F} _ {3} (\mathrm {c}) + \mathrm {H} _ {2} (\mathrm {g}), \tag {7} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\log K _ {7} (\mathrm {N i}) = \left[ - 8. 9 8 + 7. 5 8 \left(1 0 ^ {3} / T\right) \right] \pm 0. 0 6, \tag {7a}
$$

$$
\log K _ {7} (\mathrm {F e}) = \left[ - 7. 9 6 + 1 0. 0 4 \left(1 0 ^ {3} / T\right) \right] \pm 0. 0 5. \tag {7b}
$$

Assuming that the activities of nickel and iron in Hastelloy N are $\sim 0.70$ and $\sim 0.05$ , respectively, that the activity of NaF in the coolant salt is $\sim 0.08$ (for the eutectic composition), and that $P_{\mathrm{H_2}} = 1$ atm, from (7a) and (7b) we may calculate the partial pressures of HF at which nickel and iron will be oxidized to $\mathrm{NaNiF_3}$ and $\mathrm{NaFeF_3}$ , respectively, in $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ -NaF eutectic:

$$
\log P _ {\mathbf {H} \mathbf {F}} (\mathrm {N i}) = 5. 1 2 - 3. 7 9 (1 0 ^ {3} / T), \tag {8a}
$$

$$
\log P _ {\mathrm {H F}} (\mathrm {F e}) = 5. 1 8 - 5. 0 2 \left(1 0 ^ {3} / T\right). \tag {8b}
$$

A graphical representation of (8a) and (8b) is shown in Fig. 6.2.

Although the above new results are not significantly different from those reported previously, they are more accurate.

# 7. Behavior of Simulated Fission Products

# 7.1 EFFECTS OF SELECTED FISSION PRODUCTS ON METALS

J. H. Shaffer W. P. Teichert W. R. Grimes

A joint program of the Reactor Chemistry and the Metals and Ceramics Divisions has been directed toward identification of those fission products that are capable of producing superficial grain boundary cracks in Hastelloy N and investigation of other alloys of interest to the Molten-Salt Reactor Program that might be more resistive to this corrosive process. As previously reported,1 corrosive effects on Hastelloy N, not unlike those found in the MSRE fuel system, have been produced on metal tensile specimens exposed to relatively low concentrations of tellurium vapors at $650^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ for 1000 hr. Similar tests with selenium, sulfur (included for comparative purposes), iodine, cadmium, arsenic, and antimony failed to show any detectable attack on the alloy. These findings are based on metallographic examinations of the exposed tensile specimens following their stress to failure at room temperature. Although tests with all these solute elements have been continued to investigate long-term effects, the interim program has been directed toward a more complete evaluation of the implied contribution by tellurium to the corrosion process.

The experimental method has consisted of a vapor deposition technique by which the metallurgical tensile specimens were exposed to tared quantities of tellurium metal in evacuated quartz ampuls. Prior to the addition of tellurium, the quartz tubes, with specimens inserted, were evacuated of gases while heating to about $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ for removal of absorbed water. The deposition of tellurium on the specimens was accomplished by vapor transport while heating the ampuls to selected experimental temperatures of 550 to $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ at controlled rates. Tellurium additions to the quartz ampuls were based on its assumed uniform dispersion on the metal surfaces and diffusion to a depth of 5 mils into the surface layer. Tellurium concentrations of 100, 300, 1000, and 10,000 ppm by weight in the surface layer, which corresponded to initial surface populations of about $5.3 \times 10^{16}$ , $1.6 \times 10^{17}$ , and $5.3 \times 10^{18}$ atoms Te per square centimeter, respectively, have been used

for the various tests in the program. The lower concentration of $100\mathrm{ppm}$ approximates concentrations of tellurium found on metal surfaces of the MSRE fuel system, and the highest concentration has been calculated to represent the expected tellurium exposure of an MSBR fuel system during its proposed 30-year life. Although these tests have been conducted at applicable temperatures and tellurium concentrations, time at temperature and tellurium deposition rates have not corresponded to reactor operating conditions. Consequently, the program has been directed toward comparative analyses of the effects by tellurium between Hastelloy N and other alloys that might be of interest to the MSRP. The selectivity of this potential corrosion process was established by tests that showed severe cracking of Hastelloy N under experimental conditions that failed to produce recognizable attack on type 304L stainless steel and that produced only very mild attack on pure nickel.

Experimental conditions that would yield analytically significant results for this comparative evaluation study were based on a study of the time at temperature dependence of the corrosion process on Hastelloy N. Specimens exposed to 10,000 ppm of tellurium at $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ were examined at several time intervals from 20 to 1000 hr. The results showed that recognizable grain boundary cracking occurred within the first 20-hr period but that the severity of the attack increased at less than a linear rate dependence on time at temperature. Specimens withdrawn after 1000 hr under these test conditions showed effects very similar to those found on Hastelloy N samples taken from the MSRE during postoperational examinations of components from the fuel circuit.

Comparative evaluations of metals exposed to 10,000 ppm of tellurium for $100\mathrm{hr}$ at $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ have been obtained from eight experiments. Some 71 specimens which represent 14 different alloys or metals and about 24 modifications of Hastelloy N were tested. In addition to the analytical procedure described, representative samples were also provided for examinations by Auger spectroscopic and x-ray-diffraction techniques. Significant metallurgical findings from analyses of this specimen group are reported by H. E. McCoy in Chap. 10 of this report. The results showed generally that the severity of cracking was greatly reduced in alloys that were rich in chromium but that minor composition changes of Hastelloy N yielded no significant improvement in resistance to cracking.

Because of the severity of these analytical tests and the qualitative nature of the results, additional tests are being conducted with a reduced tellurium concentration of $1000\mathrm{ppm}$ for 100-hr exposures at $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Preliminary results obtained thus far from these tests indicate that the rate of tellurium deposition may be an important factor in the corrosive process. Additional long-term experiments will provide some control over this experimental variable by using thermal transpiration techniques.

# 7.2 STABILITY AND VOLATILITY OF METAL TELLURIDES

C.F.Weaver J.D.Redman

Examination $^{2,3}$ of Hastelloy N specimens from components of the Molten-Salt Reactor Experiment has shown grain boundary cracks on metal surfaces that were exposed to the fuel salt mixture during operation. Recent studies $^{4,5}$ have suggested that tellurium may play an important part in the grain boundary corrosion of Hastelloy N and, perhaps, of other alloys. $^{6}$ Consequently, the reactions, stability, and volatility of tellurium fluorides $^{7,8}$ and of structural metal tellurides $^{8}$ are being investigated by mass spectroscopy, x-ray analysis, $^{9}$ and synthesis.

Two of the three known tellurium fluorides $^{10}$ were produced by the fluorination of Te, $\mathrm{NiTe}_2$ , and $\mathrm{Ni}_3\mathrm{Te}_2$ with $\mathrm{CoF}_3$ . The results of the reaction of $\mathrm{CoF}_3$ with Te as well as the mass spectrometric cracking patterns of $\mathrm{TeF}_6$ , $\mathrm{TeF}_4$ , $\mathrm{CoF}_3$ , and tellurium were described earlier. $^{7,8}$ The analogous reactions with $\mathrm{NiTe}_2$ and $\mathrm{Ni}_3\mathrm{Te}_2$ have been observed to proceed in a similar

fashion but with lower pressures as expected. $\mathrm{CoF}_3$ and $\mathrm{NiTe_2}$ produced both $\mathrm{TeF_4}$ and $\mathrm{TeF_6}$ at temperatures greater than $160^{\circ}$ . The excess $\mathrm{NiTe_2}$ was converted to Te vapor and solid $\mathrm{Ni}_3\mathrm{Te}_2$ as the temperature increased to a final value of $780^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . The $\mathrm{CoF_3}$ and $\mathrm{Ni}_3\mathrm{Te}_2$ produced no vapors below $450^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . From 450 to $550^{\circ}$ both $\mathrm{TeF_6}$ and $\mathrm{CoF_3}$ were evolved. At $600^{\circ}\mathrm{TeF_4}$ , $\mathrm{TeF_6}$ , and $\mathrm{CoF_3}$ were observed. At higher temperatures complex mixtures formed containing unidentified species, $\mathrm{TeF_4}$ , $\mathrm{Te_2}$ , $\mathrm{CoF_3}$ , $\mathrm{CoF_2}$ , and $\mathrm{NiF_2}$ . The cracking pattern for the $\mathrm{CoF_2}$ was observed to be:

<table><tr><td>Ion</td><td>Relative intensity</td></tr><tr><td>CoF2+</td><td>35</td></tr><tr><td>CoF+</td><td>100</td></tr><tr><td>Co+</td><td>25</td></tr></table>

It was noted that the fluorinating agent $\mathrm{CoF}_3$ tends to adsorb rather large amounts of $\mathrm{O}_2$ and smaller quantities of $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ and $\mathrm{CO}_2$ during exposure for several months in a dry box containing He dried with liquid $\mathbf{N}_2$ and with molecular sieve but containing no oxygen-removing reagent. Exposure to laboratory air produced essentially the same results in about 2 hr, causing a $1.2\%$ weight gain. Baking this material for several hours in vacuum at $175^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ apparently removed these impurities completely. The use of this degassed material to $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ produced no oxides or oxyfluorides in either the vapor or the residues.

Tellurides of Ni, Fe, Cr, Mo, Bi, and Li are presently being studied. Three tellurides of nickel, $\mathrm{NiTe}_2$ , $\mathrm{NiTe}$ , and $\mathrm{Ni}_3\mathrm{Te}_2$ , have been observed. Tellurium vapor reacted with nickel metal to form $\mathrm{NiTe}_2$ at $412^{\circ}$ , $\mathrm{NiTe}$ in the range 550 to $600^{\circ}$ , and $\mathrm{Ni}_3\mathrm{Te}_2$ in the range 600 to $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . The $\mathrm{Ni}_3\mathrm{Te}_2$ , which is apparently the most stable telluride of nickel, was found to slowly decompose at $800^{\circ}$ to form nickel metal and tellurium vapor. The pressures of monomer and dimer were 7 and $4 \times 10^{-4}$ torr respectively. These pressures yield $\Delta F_{800}^{\circ}$ of +30.8 and +59.3 kcal for the reactions:

$$
\mathrm {N i} _ {3} \mathrm {T e} _ {2} (\mathrm {c}) \xrightarrow {8 0 0 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {C}} 3 \mathrm {N i} (\mathrm {c}) + \mathrm {T e} _ {2} (\mathrm {g})
$$

and

$$
\mathrm {N i} _ {3} \mathrm {T e} _ {2} (\mathrm {c}) \xrightarrow {8 0 0 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {C}} 3 \mathrm {N i} (\mathrm {c}) + 2 \mathrm {T e} (\mathrm {g})
$$

respectively. The reaction

$$
3 \mathrm {N i T e} \xrightarrow {7 0 5 ^ {\circ} \mathrm {C}} \mathrm {N i} _ {3} \mathrm {T e} _ {2} + \frac {1}{2} \mathrm {T e} _ {2} \uparrow
$$

was used to synthesize small (gram) quantities of the $\mathrm{Ni}_3\mathrm{Te}_2$ from the commercially available NiTe. While mass spectroscopy detected only tellurium vapor evolving from NiTe at $705^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , the off-gas trap in the synthesis experiments was found to contain $\mathrm{NiTe}_2$ as well as tellurium. This suggests that NiTe decomposes at $705^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ primarily by the above reaction, but also by the reaction

$$
4 \mathrm {N i T e} (\mathrm {c}) \rightarrow \mathrm {N i} _ {3} \mathrm {T e} _ {2} (\mathrm {c}) + \mathrm {N i T e} _ {2},
$$

producing $\mathrm{NiTe}_2$ vapor with a pressure of $< 10^{-6}$ torr. This is our first evidence that a structural metal telluride may exist in the gas phase. We have no evidence that $\mathrm{Ni}_3\mathrm{Te}_2$ is thermally unstable at $705^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ over a period of several days at pressures in the range of $10^{-5}$ torr. Under the same conditions at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ the decomposition to nickel and tellurium is complete, with no evidence of compounds richer in Ni than $\mathrm{Ni}_3\mathrm{Te}_2$ .

CrTe was formed by the reaction of tellurium vapor at $412^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ and $2.4 \times 10^{-2}$ torr with chromium in a quartz container. This material was heated to $750^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ in a nickel container under hard vacuum, with no evidence

ofvolatility,decomposition,orreactionwith thenickel container.

MoTe $_2$ showed no measurable vaporization, but evolved tellurium vapor at temperatures above $750^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . At $800^{\circ}$ the tellurium pressure was near $10^{-3}$ torr, yielding $\Delta F_{800}^{\circ}$ of about $+29\mathrm{kcal}$ for the reaction

$$
\mathrm {M o T e} _ {2} (\mathrm {c}) \rightarrow \mathrm {M o} (\mathrm {c}) + \mathrm {T e} _ {2} (\mathrm {g}).
$$

FeTe was found to react with Ni at $725^{\circ}$ to form $\mathrm{Ni}_3\mathrm{Te}_2$ and Fe.

$\mathrm{Bi}_{2}\mathrm{Te}_{3}$ produced no volatile species below $450^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Between 450 and $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , however, the material was both volatile and unstable; vapor species of BiTe, Bi, $\mathrm{Bi}_{2}$ , $\mathrm{Bi}_{3}$ , Te, and $\mathrm{Te}_{2}$ were observed. The gas-phase reaction $\mathrm{Bi}_{2} + \mathrm{Te}_{2} = 2\mathrm{BiTe}$ has been discussed in the literature.[11]

$\mathrm{Li}_2\mathrm{Te}$ was found to react rapidly with air at room temperature, going completely to Te and LiOH.

# 8. Development and Evaluation of Analytical Methods

A. S. Meyer

# 8.1 IN-LINE CHEMICAL ANALYSIS OF MOLTEN FLUORIDE SALT STREAMS

J.M.Dale D.L.Manning

A. S. Meyer

We have concentrated our in-line research and development efforts on preparations for the analysis of the coolant salt in the Coolant Salt Technology Facility (CSTF). Our research studies have indicated the possibility of measuring corrosion products and an active protonic species in the $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ -NaF coolant stream.

The Salt Monitoring Vessel (SMV) that has been installed at the CSTF is equipped with six risers, each fitted with $1^{1/2}$ -in. ball valves. Two of these risers are fitted with Pyrex windows and are angled so that their axes intersect the axis of one of the vertical risers at the melt surface and thus provide for illumination and visual observation of the melt. One of the vertical ports has been reserved for surface tension experiments, and the remainder are assigned to analytical measurements. We have prepared a shielded assembly equipped with three voltammetric electrodes for insertion in one of these vertical risers. The shield is a 1-in. Inconel tube whose open end penetrates the melt to below the level of the electrodes. It can be purged with $\mathrm{He - BF}_3$ to provide fresh melt surfaces and an essentially quiescent melt around the electrodes. In a second riser we will install four voltammetric electrodes where they can be observed through the viewing ports. We plan to use the third analytical port for future potentiometric measurements with a proposed $\mathrm{LaF}_3$ membrane reference electrode that uses the potential of the $\mathrm{Fe - FeF}_2$ couple as a reference. Provision has also been made to vary the flow through the SMV from 0.15 to 2 gpm so that it will be possible to study the effects of flow on the voltammetric measurements.

A large portion of our recent research has been devoted to the studies of the proton wave described in the next section. In earlier work we had investigated the fundamental electroanalytical chemistry of iron in $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ and had observed the $\mathrm{Cr(III)}\rightarrow \mathrm{Cr}(0)$ reduction wave in a region $(\sim -1.0\mathrm{V})$ near the cathodic limit of the $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ melts.2 Recently we performed voltammetric measurements on a melt prepared from the material used to charge the CSTF in order to determine whether it presented any unusual features and to measure the increase in the diffusion coefficients of the corrosion product ions with temperature.

At $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ the melt showed the anticipated iron wave and a rather large proton wave (despite a low OH analysis and careful prepumping) and no chromium wave. On heating to $600^{\circ}$ the cathodic limit of the melt decreased several hundred millivolts at a platinum working electrode so that it was not possible to observe the reduction wave from 200 ppm of added $\mathrm{Cr(III)}$ . An even greater decrease in the limit was noted at the palladium electrode. We attribute this change in the limit to the alloying of the boron reduction product with the electrode material.

We have screened a number of possible alternate electrode materials and found that iridium, pyrolytic graphite, and copper yield resolved chromium waves at $600^{\circ}$ that are comparable to those obtained at platinum at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . On a gold electrode the chromium wave appeared about $100\mathrm{mV}$ earlier, giving better resolution from the limit apparently as a result of alloying of the deposited chromium. Silver offered no advantages, and rhenium and tantalum were too active for use in $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ melts.

Our present research cells cannot be operated at the maximum operating temperature of the CSTF $(1250^{\circ}\mathrm{F})$ . We have chosen a selection of three iridium electrodes and one each of pyrolytic graphite, copper, gold, and evacuated palladium in order to offer the best opportunity to perform chromium analyses at $1250^{\circ}\mathrm{F}$ .

# 8.2 VOLTAMMETRIC STUDIES OF PROTONATED SPECIES IN MOLTEN $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ -NaF (92-8 mole %)

D. L. Manning A. S. Meyer

We have continued studies on the voltammetric behavior of protonated species in molten fluoroborate salts at an evacuated palladium tube electrode. The initial studies were conducted with the melt contained in a graphite cell, which was then enclosed in a silica jacket. However, to eliminate any effect of silica and to achieve higher melt temperatures, a nickel cell enclosure was fabricated. Both graphite and copper cells have been utilized. Voltammograms recorded at palladium or

palladium-silver (75:25) alloy electrodes revealed a well-defined wave at about $-0.1\mathrm{V}$ vs a platinum quasi-reference electrode. The wave conforms to a reversible one-electron reduction by voltammetric and chronopotentiometric criteria. By electrolyzing at a potential beyond the peak potential of the wave, an increase in pressure was observed inside the electrode. This is evidence that the wave is due to the reduction of protons with part of the resultant hydrogen diffusing through the palladium. It was first believed that it would be possible to relate the pressure measurements to the protonic content of the melt under controlled electrolysis conditions. Experience with different electrodes, however, has revealed that the "porosity" to hydrogen among the electrodes is not necessarily constant; also, pressure measurements at a given electrode will change (usually decrease) with time in the melt. Nevertheless, it is still possible to collect gas samples by this technique for measurement of isotopic ratios of hydrogen.

Our first assumption was that the voltammogram resulted from the reduction of $-\mathrm{OH}$ as $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ at the palladium electrode. However, the voltammetric results were consistently low when compared with infrared data. Also, we observed that the wave height of the voltammogram can be significantly reduced by cooling the top of the nickel cell enclosure and can be returned to the original value by heating. We now believe that the protonic species yielding the voltammetric wave is in equilibrium with a vapor species that can be condensed at cooler temperatures. Moreover, nomarked variation in the $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OH}^-$ concentration as measured by the infrared pellet technique appeared to be associated with the marked changes in the reduction wave. We have also noted that when the vapor species is removed by sweeping with helium, the wave decreases, but recovers slowly after the sweep is discontinued. This suggests that the more electroactive species may be replenished via slow reactions of a more stable protonated component.

To explain these results we have postulated reactions such as

$$
\begin{array}{l} 2 \mathrm {N a B F} _ {3} \mathrm {O H} + \mathrm {N a B F} _ {4} \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {N a} _ {2} (\mathrm {B F} _ {3}) _ {2} \mathrm {O} \\ + \mathrm {N a F} + \mathrm {H B F} _ {3} \mathrm {O H} \rightleftharpoons \text {v a p o r}, \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {N a B F} _ {3} \mathrm {O H} + 2 \mathrm {N a B F} _ {4} \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {N a} _ {2} (\mathrm {B F} _ {3}) _ {2} \mathrm {O} \\ + \mathrm {N a F} + \mathrm {H B F} _ {4} \rightleftharpoons \text {v a p o r}, \\ \end{array}
$$

in which the melt equilibrium strongly favors the $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ species. To be consistent with our observa

tions, the diffusion coefficient of the more active species must be higher than the typical value of $10^{-6}$ $\mathrm{cm}^2/\mathrm{sec}$ in fluoride melts so that the active proton concentration is lower than we suspected.

To test these hypotheses, we added crushed chromium metal to a fluoroborate melt and followed the voltammetric wave and the $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OH}^-$ . Pertinent observations at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ are recorded below.

<table><tr><td>Time</td><td>Proton wave height (μA)</td><td>H as NaBF3OH (ppm)</td></tr><tr><td>~1 hr</td><td>3300</td><td>52</td></tr><tr><td>4 hr</td><td>500</td><td>42</td></tr><tr><td>1 day</td><td>Not detected</td><td>32</td></tr><tr><td>2 days</td><td>Not detected</td><td>29</td></tr><tr><td>8 days</td><td>Not detected</td><td>24</td></tr></table>

Of interest here is that after eight days, the $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OH}^-$ level has decreased approximately one-half, whereas the voltammogram, for practical purposes, was lost within the first day. This appears to be additional evidence that the voltammetric and infrared techniques are not observing the same protonated species.

After eight days at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ the temperature was increased to $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , and a sample was withdrawn after the melt was maintained at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ for three days. It was also discovered that the cell had developed a leak while at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . The sample indicated 47 ppm H as $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ (an increase from 24 ppm at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ) and $0.22\%$ Cr. Thus the $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ production from contamination, most probably moisture, proceeded faster than its reaction with the Cr metal. At no time, however, was a voltammetric proton wave seen, and the equilibrium potential remained cathodic (melt reducing). Acidic protons (HG, $\mathrm{HBF}_4$ , etc.) were apparently consumed rapidly by the Cr metal as they were not observed voltammetrically during the buildup of the $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ .

The $0.22\%$ Cr in the filtered sample seems high. The cathodic equilibrium would seem to indicate predominantly $\mathrm{Cr}^{2+}$ in the melt, which may be more soluble than $\mathrm{Cr}^{3+}$ . However, this is yet to be established experimentally.

After the leak was detected, the salt was frozen and the cell dismantled for inspection. A white coating analyzed to be predominantly $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ was deposited on the flanged top of the cell. The route by which the salt collected on the flanged top, about 12 in. from the surface of the melt, is not clear. The gold thermowell ( $1/4$ -in-OD tube, closed end, $\sim 1/32$ -in. wall) was coated extra thickly with green-colored salt and underneath was a bright metallic plate deposited on the gold. A sample of the metallic film was found to be chromium. This would seem to support the existence of $\mathrm{Cr(II)}$ ,

which could disproportionate at the coolest surface (in this case the thermowell) into Cr metal and $\mathrm{Cr}^{3+}$ as the salt was gradually frozen. It now seems apparent that the protonic species present and associated equilibria are more complex than our original concepts. We are continuing these studies, however, and attempts will be made to evaluate the diffusion coefficient of the electroactive protonated species so that the concentration of this substance can be determined.

# 8.3 INFRARED SPECTRAL STUDIES OF $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ -NaF

J.P.Young A.S.Meyer

There is still some confusion as to the chemical reactions and stable species of H, B, F, and O that exist in $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ -NaF (92-8 mole %). Progress in our understanding of these melts has been made, however. From infrared studies of $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ -NaF melts described in the previous reporting period, we had established the existence of $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OD}^-$ ion in the melt but had not found the route of entry of this species, whether by chemical reaction or exchange. The usefulness of the $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ coolant salt in the trapping of hydrogen isotopes (tritium) is not necessarily based on an exchange reaction with $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OH}^-$ , even though this is a possible mechanism. The studies we have carried out seem to demonstrate a chemical reaction of diffused hydrogen isotopes with the melt rather than an isotopic exchange. This apparent lack of exchange in melts, coupled with a continuing unsatisfactory correlation between the results of the deuterium exchange technique described in an earlier report and the infrared pellet technique for the determination of combined hydrogen in samples of $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ , has led to a discontinuance of the isotopic exchange method. Based on present knowledge, the apparent exchange we observed in the earlier studies was probably caused by a side chemical reaction or exchange with a species other than $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OH}^-$ . Additional confirmation of the original calibration of the infrared method has been obtained by mixing known quantities of solid $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ with solid $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ -NaF and melting these mixtures in vacuum-tight crimped copper containers maintained under isothermal conditions. In these tests, $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ additions corresponding to 25

ppm of hydrogen or less were recovered quantitatively. Less than quantitative recoveries of larger additions (e.g., 40 ppm) are probably caused by the separation of a volatile proton species which does not completely reequilibrate with the salt when it solidifies. Indeed, under nonisothermal conditions, a tan-colored viscous liquid can be condensed on a water-cooled surface (see also Sect. 8.2, this report). This liquid fumes in air and was shown by NMR analysis to contain essentially equal molar quantities of H and F. From the NMR chemical shift, it is indicated that the hydrogen is not ionic. Thus the liquid cannot be $\mathrm{HBF}_4$ or $\mathrm{HBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ but might be $\mathrm{BF}_3\cdot \mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ , $\mathrm{BF}_3\cdot 2\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ , or a compound of $\mathrm{BF}_n\mathrm{OH}_{n-3}$ . Although a very careful technique is followed, it is not possible to melt $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ or $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ . NaF mixtures of low $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OH}^-$ content (7 ppm H) in the crimped isothermal containers without causing the $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OH}^-$ concentration to increase by about 15 ppm hydrogen based on the infrared pellet analysis. It appears that this increase is caused by some other form of proton, most likely $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O}$ , that is converted to $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OH}^-$ on melting. If the salt containing 20 ppm hydrogen is ground in air and again melted, a further increase of 15 to 20 ppm is observed. If the product of the first melting is ground in an inert atmosphere box and remelted, no further increase in $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OH}^-$ concentration is observed. We had reported that sometimes we could melt samples and keep the hydrogen concentration at approximately 6 to 8 ppm. These salts were melted in a nonisothermal gas phase system of very large gas-to-melt volume ratio, where it seems likely that the $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OH}^-$ was prevented from forming or the excess was decomposed by an as yet undefined mechanism. From a consideration of spectral and electrochemical results, there are at least three kinds of protonic species that can find existence in molten or solid $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ . This fact undoubtedly is a partial cause of our inability to obtain a precise analytical determination of hydrogen in these salts by the several methods available. We plan to undertake further investigations in the preparation or synthesis of $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ melts that contain essentially no $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OH}^-$ so that the various proposed solute species and their interactions can be better studied.

# 8.4 STUDIES OF PROTONS IN MOLTEN $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$

J.P.Young J.B.Bates

G. E. Boyd

Several studies of the reaction or exchange of $\mathbf{D}_2$ or $\mathbf{H}_2$ with solute species in molten $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ - $\mathrm{NaF}$ have been carried out. Our most definitive results, thus far, have

![](images/f9816d2b4dcf94d0a3f5fc918ae211ae37b375536e68835e09a9c83c64d0a536.jpg)  
Fig. 8.1. Growth of $\mathbf{BF}_3\mathbf{OD}^-$ as $\mathbf{D}_2$ is diffused into molten $\mathbf{NaBF}_4$ - $\mathbf{NaF}$ .

been obtained from experiments in which either $\mathbf{D}_2$ or $\mathbf{H}_2$ was independently diffused through 0.004-in.-wall nickel thimbles into molten $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ -NaF contained in a $\mathrm{SiO_2}$ cell at a temperature of approximately $425^{\circ}C$ . The presence or absence of the $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OD}^-$ ion and its change in concentration was followed spectrophotometrically by changes in the intensity of its characteristic infrared absorption peak at $2690~\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ . In a $\mathrm{SiO_2}$ cell one cannot observe similar changes in the $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OH}^-$ ion since that absorption peak is masked by absorption peaks of the container. Under our experimental conditions, if $\mathbf{D}_2$ is diffused into a fresh melt, some $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OD}^-$ ion is formed initially, but within 20 min the intensity of the $2690\text{-cm}^{-1}$ peak reaches a maximum and levels off, as shown in Fig. 8.1; no further changes in the intensity of this peak are noted if the diffusion of $\mathbf{D}_2$ is continued. If $\mathbf{H}_2$ is diffused into the melt first (for 2 hr) and then $\mathbf{D}_2$ is allowed to diffuse into the melt, no $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OD}^-$ absorption is seen. These results in the $\mathrm{SiO_2}$ apparatus suggest that $\mathbf{D}_2$ does not exchange with $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OH}^-$ ion in the melt, but rather that $\mathbf{D}_2$ (or $\mathbf{H}_2$ ) is capable of reducing some species in the melt. One of the ultimate products of this reduction is $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OD}^-$ . If $\mathbf{D}_2$ is first diffused, the $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OD}^-$ ion is observed; if $\mathbf{H}_2$ is first diffused, the reduction is completed with $\mathbf{H}_2$ , and no $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OD}^-$ is seen when $\mathbf{D}_2$ is subsequently introduced. We have carried out similar studies with melts contained in the $\mathrm{LaF}_3$ cell, but the results have been inconclusive because $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ creeps out of these small cells when gas is passed into the melt.

# 8.5 ANALYSIS OF COOLANT COVER GAS

R.F.Apple A.S.Meyer

In order to calibrate transducers for the measurement of hydrolysis products in coolant cover gas, it will be necessary to measure the "water" that is present in actual or simulated cover gas streams, probably as $\mathrm{BF}_3$ hydrates. Our earlier tests have shown that the $\mathrm{BF}_3$ in such gas streams interferes with the determination of water by the Karl Fischer (K.F.) titration. It appears likely that the $\mathrm{BF}_3$ interference can be eliminated by absorbing the water (and $\mathrm{BF}_3$ ) in cold pyridine and then separating the water by azeotropic distillation. Application of this technique will require a K.F. titration of improved sensitivity. D. J. Fisher and T. R. Mueller of the Analytical Chemistry Division's Instrumentation group have designed an improved automatic coulometric titrator for this purpose. This instrument decreases the generating current as the end point is approached and provides an automatic adjustment of the compensating current that is required to maintain the titrating solution at the end point.

Although the instrument functioned according to its design specifications, we were unable to exploit its improvements because of random variation in the potential of the indicator electrode system. In the coulometric titration the sample is added to a solution of essentially depleted K.F. reagent, that is, a solution of pyridine, pyridinium iodide, excess $\mathrm{SO}_2$ , and a trace of iodine in methanol. Iodine is then generated electrolytically until the original activity of free iodine is reached. Thus each mole of water added consumes one mole of coulometrically generated iodine.

The precision of the technique is primarily limited by the precision with which the free iodine activity at the end point can be reproduced. The free iodine can be detected by measuring the potential that is developed between platinum electrodes immersed in the titrating solution and polarized with microampere currents. Where excess water is present, iodine is generated at the indicator anode, while hydrogen is generated at the cathode. Thus the potential approaches the $\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{-I}_2$ couple (typically $600~\mathrm{mV}$ ). Conversely, when a large excess of iodine is present, the cathode is depolarized, and the potential approaches zero. In practice the most sensitive region of the titration curve is found at iodine concentrations corresponding to about $200\mathrm{mV}$ potential between the electrodes, and the end-point potential is selected at about this value. The iodine concentration at the cathode, demonstrated to be the potential-determining electrode, is determined by both the rate of reduction (density of polarizing current) and the rate

the free iodine reaches the electrode. Because of turbulent flow in stirred solutions the concentration of iodine varies randomly, and variation in the potential between the electrodes becomes excessive.

We have postulated that if a rapidly rotating cathode were used the rate of iodine transport to this electrode would be determined primarily by the constant velocity of the electrode through the solution rather than by the random movements in the stirred solution. An electrode was constructed of 0.040-in. platinum wire approximately $3/8$ in. long attached at right angles to a $1/8$ -in. rotating shaft. The electrode and shaft were insulated with a combination of Teflon spaghetti and shrinkable vinyl tubing. Only the tip of the platinum was exposed, so that a relative large velocity was achieved on rotation. By a systematic variation of the parameters associated with this electrode, we have found that good results can be obtained with $7 \mathrm{~mm}$ of 0.040-in. platinum exposed and rotated at $1800 \mathrm{rpm}$ with a polarizing current of $35 \mu \mathrm{A}$ . Under these conditions the response is about $15 \mathrm{mV}$ per microgram of water in approximately $100 \mathrm{ml}$ of titrating solution, and the output voltage appears essentially free of noise on the damped meter of the instrument. In the titration of test portions of methanol containing about $500 \mu \mathrm{g}$ of water, values of $500 \pm 3 \mu \mathrm{g}$ were obtained, whereas reproducibility to better than $1 \mu \mathrm{g}$ was obtained with smaller injections ( $\sim 30 \mu \mathrm{g}$ ). It is likely that this reproducibility is now limited by the precision of our injection technique. More optimum conditions can probably be found using a mechanically improved rotating system now under construction, but these results are adequate for our present usage in determining water in coolant cover gas.

These investigations were made with a prototype system in which the electrode shaft passes through a Teflon bushing. Alignment is very critical, and after a few hours of operation some Teflon "chaff" enters the cell and affects the electrode potential by covering part of its surface. An improved bearing and shaft seal are now being fabricated.

We are testing a technique for determining the hydrogen in coolant cover gas by measuring the pressure of hydrogen as it diffuses through a heated palladium diaphragm. At equilibrium the same partial pressures of hydrogen are present on both sides of the membrane, and by using prereductive techniques it may be possible to obtain total hydrogen as well as uncombined hydrogen in an untreated gas stream. Initial tests using a hydrogen purifier equipped with a heated diffusion tube of Ag-Pd alloy showed that, while the pressure outside the tube did approach the partial

pressure of hydrogen inside the flow-through system, equilibrium was approached quite slowly, particularly when going to lower pressure. This has been attributed to a reservoir of hydrogen that dissolves at high pressure in the cool ends of the diffusion tube. We are rebuilding the system to provide a more uniform temperature for the diffusion tube.

# 8.6 SPECTRAL STUDIES OF MOLTEN SALTS

# J. P. Young

As in the past, spectral studies of solute species in molten salts of interest to the MSRP have been carried out to investigate possible analytical applications and to gain insight into the chemistry of these melts. Studies are carried out in $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ -based melts, of direct interest to the MSR, fuel salt, and alkali fluoride melts which provide change in solvent character and are also of interest in developing general knowledge of molten fluoride solution chemistry. Two further papers have been written, one published and one accepted for publication, in which our views are given on the formation and stability of high oxidation states of several solute species in the presence of $\mathrm{O}^{2-}$ and/or $\mathrm{F}^{-}$ ligands in fluoride melts.

In cooperation with F. F. Blankenship, S. Kirslis (Reactor Chemistry Division), and S. Katz (Chemical Technology Division) we are studying the possibility of using the reduction of Ce(IV) to Ce(III) with the subsequent fluorimetry of Ce(III) as an analytical method for determining the reducing power of amenable melts. Katz and Pitt<sup>10</sup> have described this technique as a very sensitive means of determining organics in aqueous solution, and we have considered using this in an inorganic melt.

As a general analytical scheme the possible determination of reducing power by Ce(III) formation from Ce(IV) seems of value; either spectral absorption or fluorimetric measurements could be made. An interesting reagent for use in such a scheme would be $\mathrm{CeO}_2$ .

Based on analogous chemical behavior, one might guess that $\mathrm{CeO_2}$ would be very insoluble in fluoride melts, while $\mathrm{Ce(III)}$ would be soluble even in the presence of $\mathrm{O}^{2-}$ . Several preliminary studies have been carried out in the solvents LiF-NaF-KF and LiF-BeF $_2$ -ZrF $_4$ . In either melt it was found that $\mathrm{Ce(III)}$ was sufficiently soluble for use in this scheme. The absorption spectra, in either melt at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , in the spectral region of 600 to $200\mathrm{nm}$ , consist of three peaks located at 292, 244, and $224\mathrm{nm}$ . The spectrum corresponds to the absorption spectrum seen for $\mathrm{Ce(III)}$ in $\mathrm{CaF_2}$ crystals. $^{11}$ No fluorescence of the melt has been observed, but the solidified salt did fluoresce. The molar absorptivity of the most intense peak at $292\mathrm{nm}$ is estimated to be 700 liters $\mathrm{mole}^{-1}\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ . Addition of $\mathrm{CeO_2}$ to a melt of LiF-NaF-KF generated some $\mathrm{Ce(III)}$ which was seen spectrally; at equilibrium some undissolved $\mathrm{CeO_2}$ still remained. Based on the absorbance of the $292\mathrm{nm}$ peak, $3\mu \mathrm{eq}$ of some material in the salt was oxidized. Under our experimental conditions, down to $0.1\mu \mathrm{mol}$ of $\mathrm{Ce(III)}$ per gram of sample could be determined by absorption spectral measurement. The method was originally proposed to be considered as an alternate method for determining very low concentrations of U(III). We envision that its use as a general reducing power indicator might be of more use, either by melt absorption measurements or melt or solid fluorescent measurements. The evidence to date would indicate that $\mathrm{CeO_2}$ is not as strong an oxidant in these melts as, for example, MnF $_3$ , but $\mathrm{CeO_2}$ should be strong enough to oxidize $\mathrm{Ni}^0$ , $\mathrm{Fe}^0$ , etc., that might be present in such melts and that in the past have created problems in analysis when present with $\mathrm{Ni}^{2+}$ and $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$ .

Tellurium is a fission product of the fuel burnup in molten-salt reactors and has been implicated in some stress cracking that has been observed in the metal alloy which has been used in the MSRE. Because of this interest in Te, spectral studies of the various oxidation states will be carried out. Initially only $\mathrm{Te}^{2-}$ or $\mathrm{Te}^0$ has been considered. It has been found, spectrally, that both ZnTe and $\mathrm{Na}_2\mathrm{Te}$ are soluble in LiF-NaF-KF and that $\mathrm{Na}_2\mathrm{Te}$ is soluble in molten LiF- $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ (ZnTe was

not investigated). The spectra of the resultant rose-colored solution probably arise from the $\mathrm{Te}^{2-}$ ion and consist of a moderately broad peak at $520\mathrm{nm}$ and a very intense absorption in the ultraviolet below 350 nm. The spectra are similar but not identical to that reported by Gruen et al. $^{12}$ for $\mathrm{Li}_2\mathrm{Te}$ and $\mathrm{Cs}_2\mathrm{Te}$ in melts that were essentially chloride media. We do not know the sensitivity of the absorbance peak at $520\mathrm{nm}$ , but we estimate the molar absorptivity to be between 25 and 50 liters $\mathrm{mole}^{-1}\mathrm{cm}^{-1}$ . The solubility of either telluride, therefore, is believed to exceed $1000\mathrm{ppm}$ in LiF-NaF-KF at $525^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . The dissolved $\mathrm{Te}^{2-}$ ion is reasonably stable in an inert atmosphere at least for 24 hr, but has been found to be easily oxidized by exposure of the melt to air, possibly an oxidation via $\mathrm{O}_2$ or by the addition of $\mathrm{Fe}^{2+}$ .

In the last report we described the spectral studies of $\mathrm{Li}_3\mathrm{Bi}$ in molten LiCl that we had undertaken in cooperation with L. Ferris and his group (Chemical Technology Division). We have now carried out further spectral studies of $\mathrm{Li}_3\mathrm{Bi}$ in LiBr and of Li-Pb alloys in contact with LiCl. To summarize our results, $\mathrm{Li}_3\mathrm{Bi}$ seems to be the ionized solute species in either molten LiCl or LiBr near the LiX melting point. The reddish-yellow solution has an essentially featureless ultraviolet absorption peak which appears similar to that obtained for $\mathrm{Li}_3\mathrm{Bi}$ in molten LiCl-LiF (80-20 mole%).[13] No absorption spectrum, or color, was observed when Li-Pb alloy was in contact with molten LiCl, and it seems probable that no unusual "plumbide" species were present in the salt melt. These results are in line with chemical analyses of samples of these melts.

# 9. Other Molten-Salt Research

# 9.1 THE DISPROPORTIONATION EQUILIBRIUM OF $\mathbf{U}\mathbf{F}_3$ SOLUTIONS IN GRAPHITE

L. O. Gilpatrick L. M. Toth

In the previous report<sup>1</sup> we showed that a $\mathrm{UC}_2$ phase was the stable carbide formed in the $\mathrm{UF}_3$ disproportionation equilibrium:

$$
4 \mathrm {U F} _ {3} (\mathrm {d}) + 2 \mathrm {C} (\text {g r a p h i t e}) \rightleftharpoons 3 \mathrm {U F} _ {4} (\mathrm {d}) + \mathrm {U C} _ {2} \tag {1}
$$

when dilute (<1 mole % UF $_3$ plus UF $_4$ ) molten fluoride solutions were equilibrated in a graphite spectrophotometric cell. In order to determine equilibrium quotients:

$$
Q ^ {\prime} = \left(\mathrm {U F} _ {4}\right) ^ {3} / \left(\mathrm {U F} _ {3}\right) ^ {4} \tag {2}
$$

for Eq. (1), the reaction has been measured by approaching the equilibrium from both directions, either by disproportionating $\mathrm{UF}_3$ solutions in a graphite cell or by reacting excess $\mathrm{UC}_2$ with dilute $\mathrm{UF}_4$ solutions. We have found that the best method of measuring equilibrium quotients is by the back reaction, and for this reason the $Q$ value is written as the reciprocal of $Q'$ [Eq. (2)] when applied to the back reaction of Eq. (1).

Because the equilibration reactions were conducted in the spectrophotometric cell, $\mathrm{UF}_3$ and $\mathrm{UF}_4$ concentrations could be monitored continuously by absorption spectroscopy. Details of this procedure have already been given<sup>1</sup> and will be reviewed in a final report.<sup>2</sup>

Extensive quantitative data have been obtained for $Q$ , for the back reaction of Eq. (1), as a function of temperature and melt composition. These data represent refinements of earlier results<sup>1</sup> as a consequence of improvements in the data analyses. These improvements are primarily due to better base-line determinations and calibration data for the absorption spectra of $\mathrm{UF}_3$ and $\mathrm{UF}_4$ . We have found that the equilibrium is more sensitive to temperature and solvent changes than was originally anticipated by previous investigators.<sup>3</sup>

In Fig. 9.1 the logarithm of $Q$ is plotted as a function of $T_{\mathbf{K}}^{-1}$ ( $T_{\mathbf{K}}$ is the Kelvin temperature) for the back reaction of Eq. (1) in the solvent LiF-BeF $_2$ (66-34 mole %). Also shown in the figure at the right ordinate is the ratio $\mathrm{UF}_3 / (\mathrm{UF}_4 + \mathrm{UF}_3)$ , equal to $R$ , and at the top of the figure is the centigrade scale. Two lines drawn through the data points represent the experimental uncertainty, which arises mainly from the base line in the absorption spectra.

Equilibria at various temperatures were approached from both the high-temperature (open circles) and low-temperature (closed circles) directions. To approach equilibrium from the high-temperature direction, the system was initially held ca. $50^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ above the temperature sought until the $\mathrm{UF}_3$ concentration ceased to grow $[\mathrm{UF}_4$ reacting with $\mathrm{UC}_2$ in the back reaction of Eq. (1)]. Then, the temperature was dropped $50^{\circ}$ and the $\mathrm{UF}_3$ concentration allowed to decrease by the forward reaction of Eq. (1) until no further change

![](images/b9fa4004f7615136216cc89e10daca6c31e1b9810a3ce714f82b823c388365fd.jpg)  
Fig. 9.1. Equilibrium quotients vs temperature for the reaction $\mathrm{UC}_2 + 3\mathrm{UF}_4 \rightleftharpoons 4\mathrm{UF}_3 + 2\mathrm{C}$ in LiF-BeF $_2$ (66-34 mole%).

occurred. Equilibrium could be shifted repeatedly in this manner by varying the temperature of the system. It is shown in Fig. 9.1 that the equilibrium is very sensitive to temperature changes, shifting by $\Delta Q = 10^{7}$ in the range from 500 to $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . This indicates that the stability of $\mathrm{UF}_3$ is increased from ca. $5\%$ at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ to ca. $60\%$ at $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

Solvent effects on the equilibrium also have been found to be very large. Changing the composition from $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}$ (66-34 mole %) to (48-52 mole %) $^4$ at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ increases the $Q$ value by $10^{5}$ , as found by comparing Fig. 9.2 with Fig. 9.1. The plot of $\log Q$ vs $1 / T_{\mathrm{K}}$ for the equilibrium of the reverse reaction for Eq. (2) in $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}$ (48-52 mole %) is similar to that of the previous figure. However, comparable concentrations of $\mathrm{UF}_3$ can be held at temperatures which are 150 to $200^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ lower than in the $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}$ (66-34 mole %) case.

The two sets of $Q$ values differ from each other simply by the ratio of activity coefficients, $\gamma$ of $\mathrm{UF}_3$ and $\mathrm{UF}_4$ in the two solvents raised to the appropriate powers. Baes has assigned a value $\gamma = 1$ to $\mathrm{UF}_3$ and $\mathrm{UF}_4$ in LiF-BeF $_2$ (66-34 mole %) and has shown on this basis that a $\gamma = 0.7$ and 10 was determined for $\mathrm{UF}_3$ and $\mathrm{UF}_4$ , respectively, in LiF-BeF $_2$ (48-52 mole %). This yields a ratio:

$$
Q _ {\mathrm {L B}} / Q _ {\mathrm {L} _ {2} \mathrm {B}} \cong 4 0 0 0.
$$

Our data for $Q_{\mathrm{LB}}$ when extrapolated linearly to $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ yield a $Q_{\mathrm{LB}} / Q_{\mathrm{L2B}} \cong 10,000$ , which agrees with Baes's predictions. It should be noted that the data of Fig. 9.2 suggest a slight curvature which if extrapolated on a nonlinear basis would yield even better agreement.

The data of Figs. 9.1 and 9.2 in the "laboratory system" of $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}$ mixtures has been used as a basis for study by which the stability of $\mathbf{U}\mathbf{F}_3$ in reactor-type solvents may be understood. Our earlier predictions were that for $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ZrF_4}$ (64-29-5 mole %) the log $Q$ vs $1 / T_{\mathrm{K}}$ plot of Fig. 9.1 would be shifted upward by ca. $Q = 10$ for the entire temperature range. This shift is due to the $\mathrm{ZrF_4}$ , which decreases the fluoride ion concentration and causes an increase in the $\mathrm{UF_3}$ stability. For $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ThF_4}$ (72-16-12 mole %) we also predicted that $\mathrm{UF_3}$ should be less stable than in $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}$ (66-34 mole %). However, equilibration measurements have recently been performed as described for the $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}$ solvent systems, and they

![](images/ee94fafa1f62f1cb53f4d62272921984772eb0ada54589cd8d27da4cfcd7f5b6.jpg)  
Fig. 9.2. Equilibrium quotients vs temperature for the reaction $\mathrm{UC}_2 + 3\mathrm{UF}_4 \rightleftharpoons 4\mathrm{UF}_3 + 2\mathrm{C}$ in LiF-BeF $_2$ (48-52 mole%).

show definitely that $\mathrm{UF}_3$ is at least as stable as in the LiF-BeF $_2$ (66-34 mole %) plot of Fig. 9.1. A detailed log $Q$ vs $1 / T_{\mathrm{K}}$ plot for LiF-BeF $_2$ -ThF $_4$ (72-16-12 mole %) will be presented later. $^2$

# 9.2 EMF STUDIES OF OXIDE EQUILIBRIA IN MOLTEN FLUORIDES

R.G.Ross C.E.Bamberger C.F.Baes,Jr.

Oxide equilibrium studies have continued because of their relevance to MSBR fuel reprocessing development and to its oxide tolerance. Previously the solubility product for $\mathrm{ThO_2}$ has been estimated from $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ and $\mathrm{PaO_2}$ solubility measurements,7 and the solubility product for NiO has been estimated from measurements in LiF-BeF solutions.8 However, these estimates have not been corroborated by direct measurement.

D. D. Sood's<sup>9</sup> attempts to determine the $\mathrm{ThO}_2$ solubility product by directly measuring the oxide in solution were not satisfactory because the oxide analyses had considerable scatter. It seemed desirable, therefore, to utilize a method not solely dependent on oxide analyses. Thus, we have initiated emf studies of the equilibrium

$$
\mathrm {T h O} _ {2} (\mathrm {c}) + \mathrm {N i F} _ {2} (\mathrm {d}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {T h F} _ {4} (\mathrm {d}) + \mathrm {N i O} (\mathrm {c}) \tag {1}
$$

in $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ThF_4}$ (initially 72-16-12 mole $\%$ ). Through a combination of the emf measurements and material balance we expect to obtain precise values for the solubility products of $\mathrm{ThO_2}$ and NiO in these melts. In addition, by varying the composition of the melt, we will obtain a measure of the activity coefficients of $\mathrm{Ni^{2 + }}$ and $\mathrm{Th}^{4 + }$ , which can be compared with previous estimates.10

The $\mathrm{NiF}_2$ concentration in these experiments is followed potentiometrically with a reference electrode designed by Bronstein and Manning. In this electrode, $\mathrm{Li}_2\mathrm{BeF}_4$ saturated with $\mathrm{NiF}_2$ is contained in a single-crystal $\mathrm{LaF}_3$ cup. Efforts to date have been primarily concerned with the design and assembly of equipment, development of analytical and handling techniques, the preparation of "oxide free" starting materials, and elimination of design inadequacies associated with the $\mathrm{LaF}_3$ reference electrode.

We have been able to prepare salt which, based on HF evolved during stripping after hydrofluorination and the equilibrium

$$
\mathrm {O H} ^ {-} + \mathrm {F} ^ {-} (\mathrm {d}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {O} ^ {2 -} (\mathrm {d}) + \mathrm {H F} (\mathrm {g}), \tag {2}
$$

contained 20 ppm oxide. A direct analysis (using a refined $\mathrm{KBrF_4}$ fusion method) gave an oxide concentration of $31\pm 3$ ppm. We feel that this is quite good agreement, particularly since the equilibrium quotient for the above reaction was estimated from a value for $\mathrm{Li}_2\mathrm{BeF}_4$ . In addition, it reflects our ability to avoid contamination of the samples in handling prior to analysis. The $\mathrm{LaF_3}$ reference electrode has been modified a number of times to eliminate problems such as crystal fracture, loss of electrode contact, creeping of salt, and shorting of insulators.

In the several runs to date, the initial potential has been stable and approximately the value we expected. As reliability of the electrode is demonstrated, measurement of equilibrium (1) will be completed, and emf measurements will be extended to other oxide-fluoride equilibria of the general type

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {M F} _ {x} (\mathrm {d}) + \frac {y}{2} \mathrm {N i O} (\mathrm {c}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {M O} _ {y / 2} (\mathrm {c}) \\ + \frac {x}{2} \mathrm {N i F} _ {2} (\mathrm {d}) + \frac {y - x}{2} \mathrm {N i} ^ {0} (\mathrm {c}), \\ \end{array}
$$

wherein the dissolved fluoride $\mathbf{MF}_x$ may be $\mathbf{U}\mathbf{F}_4$ , $\mathbf{ThF}_4$ , $\mathbf{ZrF}_4$ , $\mathbf{T_iF_4}$ , or $\mathbf{CrF}_2$ and the corresponding oxide is, respectively, $\mathbf{UO}_2$ , $\mathbf{ThO}_2$ , $\mathbf{ZrO}_2$ , $\mathbf{T_iO}_2$ , or $\mathbf{Cr}_2\mathbf{O}_3$ .

# 9.3 THE LITHIUM FLUORIDE-TETRAFLUOROBORATE PHASE DIAGRAM

A.S.Dworkin M.A.Bredig

Determination of this phase diagram rounded out the studies of the alkali metal fluoride-tetrafluoroborate binary systems at ORNL. A comparison of activity coefficients calculated from Fig. 9.3 with those for the sodium, potassium, and rubidium systems calculated by Moulton and Braunstein $^{12}$ shows an increasing positive deviation from ideality with decreasing cation size. At $1000^{\circ}\mathrm{K}$ and mole fraction $X_{\mathrm{BF}_4}^{-}$ of 0.2, 0.4, and 0.6, activity coefficients $\gamma_{\mathrm{LiF}}$ were 1.16, 1.32, and 1.40, while values of $\gamma_{\mathrm{NaF}}$ were 1.10, 1.20, and 1.23. The

![](images/44b7f6eb8bea15d5f8191dd1f649f5b30e43eb517528c2df7d50aa5ee932c1fb.jpg)  
Fig. 9.3. Phase diagram of the system LiF-LiBF $_4$ .

potassium system shows very slight positive deviation, while the rubidium system is essentially ideal. This trend most likely reflects the smaller distance and consequently greater multipole interaction between $\mathrm{BF}_4^-$ ions in the presence of smaller cations.

# 9.4 CHRONOPOTENTIOMETRY BASED ON DIFFUSION OF MOBILE NONELECTROACTIVE SPECIES

J.Braunstein H.R.Bronstein J.Truitt

It has previously been demonstrated that the electrical transference number of $\mathrm{Be(II)}$ relative to fluoride ion as the reference constituent is zero in molten LiF- $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ mixtures at compositions between 0.3 and 0.7 mole fraction $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ ,[13] and probably beyond. We have pointed out[14] that if alkali ions remain the sole current carriers (relative to fluoride) in these systems as the $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ content is increased (and the $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ network structure develops), a novel and potentially useful response to electrochemical scanning methods is expected. We also demonstrated the existence of a well-defined chronopotentiometric transition time for a beryllium metal anode in molten NaF- $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ (75 mole $\%$ $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ ) and interpreted it qualitatively as due to the formation of a nonconductive film of $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ at the electrode surface.[14]

We here present the results of a quantitative interpretation of the chronopotentiograms based on the diffusion of $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ formed at the beryllium electrode surface into the melt (interdiffusion of $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ and $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ -alkali fluoride mixtures).

The potential of a beryllium anode measured relative to a beryllium reference electrode in melt of uniform composition during a constant-current electrolysis rises sharply as it sees increasingly high $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ concentrations, the time required for the rise being governed by the current density and the interdiffusion of $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ and alkali fluoride. The reversible emf of the resulting concentration cell, I:

$$
\mathrm {B e} \left| \begin{array}{l l} \mathrm {B e F} _ {2} - \mathrm {M F} & \mathrm {B e F} _ {2} - \mathrm {M F} \\ y _ {0} & y _ {0} + \Delta y = y \end{array} \right| \mathrm {B e} \quad \mathrm {I}
$$

reference polarized electrode

is13

$$
2 F E = \int_ {y _ {0}} ^ {y} (t - t _ {\mathrm {B e} ^ {2 +}}) \frac {1 + y}{1 - y} \frac {\partial \mu_ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}}}{\partial y} d y. \tag {1}
$$

If $t_{\mathrm{Be}}$ is zero, the cell emf becomes

$$
\frac {2 F E}{R T} = \ln \frac {y}{(1 - y) ^ {2}} - \ln \frac {y _ {0}}{(1 - y _ {0}) ^ {2}} + \frac {2 F E ^ {\mathrm {E}}}{R T}. \tag {2a}
$$

In the above equation, $y$ and $\mu_{\mathrm{BeF}_2}$ are the mole fraction and chemical potential of $\mathrm{BeF}_2$ , $y_0$ is the initially uniform melt composition, and $t_\mathrm{Be}$ is the transference number of $\mathrm{Be(II)}$ . The first two terms on the right-hand side of (2a) give the "ideal" emf, and $E^{\mathbf{E}}$ is the "excess" cell emf, which can be calculated if the activity coefficients are known:

$$
\frac {2 F E ^ {\mathrm {E}}}{R T} = \int_ {y _ {0}} ^ {y} (1 - t _ {\mathrm {B e} ^ {2 +}}) \frac {1 + y}{1 - y} \frac {\mathrm {d} \ln \gamma_ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}}}{\mathrm {d} y} \mathrm {d} y. \quad (2 b)
$$

If only the alkali ions are mobile and carry electrical current by migration from the anode under the influence of the electric field, the Faradaic current can be sustained only by alkali ions that diffuse back to the anode. The situation is analogous to chronopotentiometry of a dilute electroactive constituent whose electrolysis product is soluble in the solution; however, the emf is given not by the Nernst equation, but by the expression for the binary molten salt concentration cell, Eq. (1) or (2). The solution of Fick's second law,

$$
\frac {\partial C _ {M ^ {+}}}{\partial t} = D \frac {\partial^ {2} C _ {M ^ {+}}}{\partial X ^ {2}},
$$

is subject to the usual initial condition

$$
\{t = 0 \} \rightarrow \left\{C _ {M ^ {+}} = C ^ {0} _ {M ^ {+}}, 0 <   X <   \infty \right\} \tag {i}
$$

(i.e., uniform concentration initially) and to the boundary conditions

$$
\{X = \infty \} \rightarrow \left\{C _ {\mathrm {M} ^ {+}} = C ^ {0} _ {\mathrm {M} ^ {+}}, 0 <   t <   \infty \right\} \tag {ii}
$$

[uniform (initial) concentration at infinite distance from the electrode] and

$$
\frac {i}{F A} = J _ {\mathrm {M} ^ {+}} \left| _ {X = 0} = - D \frac {\partial C _ {\mathrm {M}} ^ {+}}{\partial X} \right| _ {X = 0} \tag {iii}
$$

(electric current limited by back diffusion of mobile species). The third condition is unusual in that it is a consequence of depletion of a nonelectroactive constituent, alkali ion, by migration rather than the usual depletion of electroactive species by electrode reaction.

The solution of the diffusion equation with the above initial and boundary conditions is, however, formally identical with the solution for ordinary chronopotentiometry, and the concentration at the electrode surface<sup>15</sup> is

$$
C _ {\mathrm {M F}} = C ^ {0} _ {\mathrm {M F}} - \alpha t ^ {1 / 2}, \tag {3}
$$

where $C^0$ and $C$ are the initial and the instantaneous concentrations at the electrode surface and

$$
\alpha = \frac {2}{\sqrt {\pi}} \frac {1 / A}{F \sqrt {D}}.
$$

The beryllium concentration at the electrode is, by stoichiometry,

$$
C _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} = C ^ {0} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} + 2 \frac {\bar {V} _ {\mathrm {M F}}}{\bar {V} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}}} \alpha t ^ {1 / 2}. \tag {4}
$$

The concentrations are in equivalents per cubic centimeter, and the $\overline{V}$ are partial molal volumes.

The transition time resulting from setting $y = 1$ in Eq. (2) is identical with that of the Sand equation:

$$
\alpha \tau^ {1 / 2} = C ^ {0} _ {\mathrm {M F}} = \frac {1 - y _ {0}}{\bar {V} _ {\mathrm {M F}} + (\bar {V} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} - \bar {V} _ {\mathrm {M F}}) y _ {0}}. \tag {5}
$$

The chronopotentiogram may be calculated, with the probably poor assumption for these melts of a concentration-independent diffusion coefficient, by combining Eqs. (2), (4), and (5), making use of the relations among the concentrations:

$$
C _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} = \frac {2 y}{\bar {V} _ {\mathrm {M F}} + (\bar {V} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} - \bar {V} _ {\mathrm {M F}}) y} = \frac {2 y}{V _ {m}},
$$

$$
C _ {\mathrm {M F}} = \frac {1 - y}{V _ {m}}; \tag {6a}
$$

$$
\frac {2 F E ^ {\text {i d e a l}}}{R T} = \ln \frac {(1 - \bar {V} _ {\mathrm {M F}} C _ {\mathrm {M F}}) [ 1 + (\bar {V} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} - \bar {V} _ {\mathrm {M F}}) C _ {\mathrm {M F}} ]}{(\bar {V} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} C _ {\mathrm {M F}}) ^ {2}}
$$

$$
- \ln \frac {\left(1 - \bar {V} _ {\mathrm {M F}} C ^ {0} _ {\mathrm {M F}}\right) \left[ 1 + \left(\bar {V} _ {\mathrm {B}} - \bar {V} _ {\mathrm {M F}}\right) C ^ {0} _ {\mathrm {M F}} \right]}{\left[ \bar {V} _ {\mathrm {B e F} _ {2}} C ^ {0} _ {\mathrm {M F}} \right] ^ {2}}. \tag {6b}
$$

![](images/3691776d24ec49b8f8e7442f1e4519e8be7332138d5088e955eb7a6bdf78375a.jpg)  
Fig. 9.4. Chronopotentiogram in molten $\mathrm{NaF - BeF_2}$ (about 25-75 mole $\%$ ) at $520^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ , current density about $40\mathrm{mA / cm}^2$ . Potential of beryllium working electrode, relative to a beryllium reference electrode, vs time. Dashed lines are calculated from the transition time $\tau = 170$ sec.

The excess emf for the system $\mathrm{NaF - BeF_2}$ was estimated from activity coefficient data in LiF-BeF $_2$ mixtures<sup>16</sup> since extensive data for $\mathrm{NaF - BeF_2}$ are not available, leading to the estimate from Eq. (2b) and ref. 16:

$$
\frac {2 F E ^ {\mathrm {E}}}{R T} \cong - 3. 5 \left(y - y _ {0} + \ln \frac {y}{y _ {0}}\right). \tag {7}
$$

Figure 9.4 shows a typical chronopotentiogram, measured in $0.75\mathrm{BeF}_2 - 0.25\mathrm{NaF}$ , with "ideal" and "real" ones calculated from Eqs. (1), (2), and (3-7) for a transition time of 170 sec, leading to an average diffusion coefficient of $3 \times 10^{-7}$ .

Values of $i\tau^{1/2}$ were found essentially independent of current density in the range 20 to $100\mathrm{mA/cm^2}$ . This estimate of the diffusion coefficient is two orders of magnitude smaller than normal liquid diffusion coefficients and an order of magnitude smaller than values reported in a less viscous LiF-BeF $_2$ -ZrF $_4$ mixture. $^{17}$ The present results demonstrate that a transition time is predicted for nonelectroactive species at high concentration in molten salt mixtures and is observed experimentally. A corollary of these results is that special care

may be needed in interpreting electrochemical scanning data in mixed molten salt solvents approaching unicationic conduction.

Initial measurements with $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2}$ mixtures (10-90 mole %) show the anticipated increase in transition time corresponding to the higher mobility of lithium ion. The measurements were made with an ORNL 2943 cyclic voltammeter, designed by T. R. Mueller,[18] the salts being contained in a nickel vessel in a controlled-atmosphere envelope that was heated in a controlled-temperature furnace.

Analogous equations may be developed for other molten salt systems, such as $\mathrm{AlCl}_3$ -MCl melts. The

analog of (1) and (2) for the emf of the concentration cell with aluminum electrodes is

$$
2 F E = \int_ {y _ {0}} ^ {y} (1 - t _ {\mathrm {A l}}) \frac {1 + 2 y}{1 - y} \frac {\partial \mu_ {\mathrm {A l C l} _ {3}}}{\partial y} d y,
$$

$$
\frac {2 F E ^ {\text {i d e a l}}}{R T} = \ln \frac {y}{(1 - y) ^ {3}} - \ln \frac {y _ {0}}{(1 - y _ {0}) ^ {2}}.
$$

# Part 3. Materials Development

H.E.McCoy

Our materials work is currently involved with several important areas including (1) gaining a better understanding of the intergranular cracking of Hastelloy N that occurred in the MSRE fuel system and a method of controlling this cracking in future reactors, (2) developing a graphite with improved dimensional stability under radiation, (3) development of surface sealing methods for reducing the permeability of graphite to $^{135}\mathrm{Xe}$ , (4) modification of the composition of Hastelloy N to obtain an alloy that is more resistant to neutron irradiation, (5) evaluation of Hastelloy N for use in steam generators, (6) construction of a molybdenum system for use with bismuth and fluoride salts, and (7) the evaluation of other materials for use as structural materials in the chemical processing plant.

The first area has continued to occupy a high priority, and a rather extensive program of laboratory tests has been pursued. The laboratory tests are directed at determining the cause of the cracking, the rate of progression with time and temperature, and a reasonable solution to the cracking problem. The emphasis is on the fission product tellurium, since considerable evidence exists that this element is the cause of the cracking.

The lifetime of a breeder core will be determined by the dimensional stability of the graphite. Dimensional changes can be accommodated to some extent, but volume expansion is usually accompanied by increasing permeability to xenon, which can become intolerable. Thus the problem of dimensional stability cannot be separated from the requirement of low permeability to $^{135}\mathrm{Xe}$ . We are evaluating graphites made by commercial vendors and locally in an effort to understand what types of graphite have the best dimensional

stability. This information is being fed back to commercial vendors and into our own experimental fabrication program. Pyrolytic carbon coatings derived from propene are currently being studied as a means of reducing the permeability to $^{135}\mathrm{Xe}$ . Improved methods of characterizing the coatings are being developed.

The program to develop a modified Hastelloy N with improved resistance to radiation indicated that alloys with additions of 1.5 to $2.0\%$ titanium were most promising, and recent efforts have concentrated on these. Material has been obtained from three vendors that has been made by two basic melting practices. The evaluation includes weldability tests, unirradiated mechanical property tests, and postirradiation mechanical property tests.
